# Complete Guide to SQL for Data Engineering: from Beginner to Advanced

> Are you looking to get up and running with SQL as a data engineer, but not sure where to begin? In this course, instructor Deepak Goyal provides a comprehensive overview of SQL specifically targeted at data engineers, from basic queries and analytic tasks to advanced functions and data management.Find out why SQL is one of the most frequently and widely used programming languages for data engineer

> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced) | 7h 11m | Intermediate | 48K learners
> [Jump to Path Context ↓](#path-context)

### 1. SQL Introduction

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [What is SQL and why learn it?](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/what-is-sql-and-why-learn-it?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/what-is-sql-and-why-learn-it?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Whenever you want to share thoughts with your loved ones or you want to communicate with them, you use your own native language. Similarly, whenever you want to communicate with database, the language for communication is [SQL](../../Skills/Data%20Science/SQL.md), which stands for structured query language. It allows you to talk to [Databases](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Databases.md), to store information, retrieve information, and perform powerful operations on your data. To appreciate what SQL brings to the table, I thought we should delve into a bit of history as to how we used to store and retrieve the data before it came into picture. Before SQL, the data was stored as either a paper files, or a flat files, or hierarchical databases, or in the form of network database. Imagine that you are running a successful online store and your inventory is growing faster than ever. You started with simple spreadsheet. Maybe you are using [Microsoft Excel](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Microsoft%20Excel.md) to put all your order, product, all those information into an [Excel](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Microsoft%20Excel.md). But as your store is growing, it's become a nightmare to keep track of everything in terms of an Excel.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/what-is-sql-and-why-learn-it?u=76281980&t=94)** Orders are getting lost, and customer are frustrated because you might not able to pull out the correct information. Or you might find it very difficult to manage all of these things. That's where the SQLs comes to the rescue. So such kind of a problem exist before the start of the SQL. Now, coming back again to the SQL, SQL is a programming language which is used for controlling and manipulating the structured data, which is present in your databases. SQL allows Juniper to manipulate the data present in databases with the help of queries. And the good thing is that these queries is a kind of an high-level language. That means it's a very human friendly. It is not as complex as writing the programming language, for example, C, C++, [Java](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Java.md). So SQL, it's quite easy to comprehend. SQL serves as standard database language for many relational [Database Management](../../Topics/Database%20Management.md) systems. For example, [MySQL](../../Skills/Software%20Development/MySQL.md), MS Access, Oracle, and many more. Now as the name comes in, RBDMS, a question might have popped up in your brain that what does this RBDMS stands for?
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/what-is-sql-and-why-learn-it?u=76281980&t=188)** So RBDMS, it stands for relational database management system. A relational database is a form of a database that stores the data in terms of a table so that it can be used in relationship with other tables. So in RDBMS, you create multiple tables, and this table have some kind of relationship between them, and that's why this entire system is called as RBDMS, that is relational database management system. For example, imagine that you are holding the information about a company. Now in a company, you have an entities like employee. In that case, you store the information of the employee in an employee table. Similarly, you will have a department also in the company, and department table keeps a track of all the departments. Now every employee is a part of a sum department, right? So it belongs to a specific department, and that's why there is a relationship between employee and department table, and that's how the relationship is built. So this is about the SQL. Now the question comes in why you should learn SQL. So SQL is a universal language.
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/what-is-sql-and-why-learn-it?u=76281980&t=287)** The best part with the SQL is SQL is not a database specific, as we saw in the previous slide. That SQL is common for multiple databases, whether it is MS SQL, or either MySQL or IBM Db2 or [Postgres](../../Skills/Software%20Development/PostgreSQL.md). So SQL is quite similar everywhere. Hence, knowing an SQL helps you to work with any kind of databases. SQL is very efficient in handling the huge amounts of data. Your databases holds the structured data, the data which has a sum fixed schema. For example, when you store the data into the database, you define the tables, and table has a sum fixed format, like every table have some specific set of column and their data types. So handling such kind of an structured data is very easy to do with SQL. And even SQL can manage such a structured data even without worrying about the size of this data. So whether you have a small size of a data or you have a bigger size of a data, SQL can handle all these types of data. Another advantage of learning the SQL is business application implementation.
>
> **[6:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/what-is-sql-and-why-learn-it?u=76281980&t=381)** Many business applications and software systems rely on databases to store and retrieve the data. SQL is the language that facilitates that seamless connections between applications and databases and making it essential for the developers and IT professionals. Another reason could be is [Scalability](../../Skills/Artificial%20Intelligence%20(AI)/Scalability.md). As the data volumes grows, the importance of having the scalable solution is important. SQL knowledge helps in writing efficient queries and optimizing database performance, ensuring that the system can scale to handle increased amounts of data. Last but not the least, it helps in the job opportunities. Many job roles in the field of data analysis, [business intelligence](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Business%20Intelligence%20(BI).md), [Web Development](../../Topics/Web%20Development.md), and software engineering, all of them requires SQL proficiency. Learning SQL opens up a wide range of career opportunities and enhances your marketability in the job market. So by now you have understood the very important role of the SQL. Now in the next session, we are going to deep dive into an importance of SQL in the world of the [Data Engineering](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Data%20Engineering.md). And we will see how SQL can play a significant role
>
> **[8:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/what-is-sql-and-why-learn-it?u=76281980&t=480)** in making your career in data engineering.

#### [Role of SQL in data engineering](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/role-of-sql-in-data-engineering?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/role-of-sql-in-data-engineering?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Before understanding the use of [SQL](../../Skills/Data%20Science/SQL.md) in [Data Engineering](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Data%20Engineering.md), we first need to understand what is the role of data engineer, why it is important for them. So let's understand the day in, day out responsibilities of the data engineer. The first and foremost important role of a data engineer is to finding the key insights. Data engineers are expected to find the insights from the data which is available to them in the form of [Databases](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Databases.md). They have to write a queries to get the analytics out of it. Another important role of a data engineer is to interpret the data. Data is always try to tell some kind of a story. The work of a data engineer is to interpret that story and identify what this data is trying to tell, but doing so they need to do a lot of things. Sometimes the data which is given to them is not of a very good form. It may have a lot of null values, it might have a lot of duplicate values, it might have a lot of unwanted columns, so data engineer has to clean this data before doing any analytics on it. So another aspect of a data engineer is to use the SQL
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/role-of-sql-in-data-engineering?u=76281980&t=97)** to do these kind of cleaning and analytics. Another important role of a data engineer is to design the ETL process. Now, when the [word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) comes ETL process, a question pops up in the mind, what is ETL? You might have heard of it, this ETL, or aware of it, but let's have our quick revision of what is this ETL process. ETL process is stands for extract, transform, and load. In data world, many times you get into a situation where you have been asked to move the data from one source to another data source. A pretty good example is moving the data from your transactional databases to the data warehouses. Now, whenever you move the data from this transaction data source to a data warehouse data source, you design an ETL pipeline or ETL process. That's first, extract the data from the source, you might clean, you might transform, and then you save the result into the destination. That entire process is called as ETL. There are a lot of ETL tools available in the market, for example, Informatica, SSIS, [Azure](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Azure.md) Data Factory. SQL is the backbone of the data engineers.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/role-of-sql-in-data-engineering?u=76281980&t=194)** SQL plays a very crucial role in managing and manipulating the large volumes of the data. And as data engineers has to play with data, the SQL is of a prime importance for them. If you go a little deeper down, you will find that data engineers has to do the database designing as well. When I say database designing, they have to form the data models, they have to decide what are the number of tables, what could be the name of these tables, how could they relate these multiple tables? So again, they will use the SQL to design this database solution. Sometimes they have to do the [Data Ingestion](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Data%20Ingestion.md). It's not always that the source is your SQL system. Sometimes they have a variety of different system, maybe CSV file, maybe [JSON](../../Skills/Web%20Development/JSON.md) file, maybe data from SFTP server. How you can ingest the data into the database from this variety of data sources? Again, all of these will require the SQL knowledge. As we already talked, they need to do the transformations as well, right? Change the data from one form to another form. For example, you have a normal table and they might have to create another table, which is of an aggregated form. For example, you have a sales table, which has the sales for every day,
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/role-of-sql-in-data-engineering?u=76281980&t=286)** but you want to showcase the sales on a monthly basis, so you might going to do the [Data Transformation](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Data%20Transformation.md) where you aggregate the data. Sometimes they have to integrate the data from variety of sources, or they might have to do the performance optimization so that the queries which is running in the system might run faster. Hence, overall, the SQL plays a very critical role for the data engineers as data engineer has to play with the data. Hence for knowing an SQL and having the proficiency in the SQL is of prime importance for the data engineers. In the next session, we are going to start installing our test environment where we can go and play with our SQL. If you are using [GitHub](../../Skills/Software%20Development/GitHub.md) [Codespaces](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Codespaces.md), you can skip the next two movies in the course and move directly to the chapter two, Introduction to the Database and Tables.

I know you must have this question in your mind. A database is like a well-organized digital filing cabinet. There, we store information. It's like a place to keep our data in an orderly manner, so that we can easily find, manage, and update it whenever needed. If we talk in more technical terms, a database is a structured collection of a data that is organized and stored in a way that facilitates efficient retrieval, management, and updation of information. Let's take a one real life example to understand the database. Imagine that if we have a one smart shopping application where people are buying their grocery items using this online app. Now, this online app can have a database that is a smart shopping database. In this database we can have tables. Tables is used to organize your data in a relational manner. Here, imagine that you have a different [products](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Microsoft%20Products.md). For example, you have fruit juice, you have electronic items, you have clothes, you have toys.

Now this categories table will have a two column. One is we call it id, and this will be let's say have a serial primary key. Now you must be thinking what is the serial primary key? Now this serial primary key is like autogenerated IDs. So this will be an autogenerated ID. Whenever you insert any new row into this category table, automatically, a sequential number get started. For example, for first row, the ID will become one. For second row, it'll become two, and so on, so forth. So instead of this just id, let me give this name as category_id. Now, I want to have a one more column and let's say column name is name, which is storing the category name. And the data type is varchar.

It means that if there 100 characters you try to store as the name, it'll take the space of 100 characters. But if you have a less number of characters in your name, then it will occupy less space. We are restricting that, the maximum width would be 100 characters and that's it. Now click on this execute script button. What it's saying, there is one error. #### [Combining conditions with AND and OR](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/combining-conditions-with-and-or?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/combining-conditions-with-and-or?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Assume that you want to study, but whenever you want to study, you want to have the free time and a course available. If both of these things are available to you, a free time and course, then only you will study. Now this is nothing but a situation of a logical conditioning. Exactly, many times in your business you get into those conditioning and at that time, [SQL](../../Skills/Data%20Science/SQL.md) can help you to retrieve the data based on those conditions. And it can be done using logical operators. The two most common logical operators in SQL is AND and OR. As the name suggests, AND operator, it's something which try to pull the values or become true when the left hand side condition and the right hand side condition, both condition are true. For example, you say you study when course is available and when free time is also available. Now imagine that in this situation, if you have a free time but course is not available or you have course but free time is not available. In both of these situations, you will not study.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/combining-conditions-with-and-or?u=76281980&t=93)** You will only and only study when both of these two things are available or both of these two things are true. Similarly, there is one more logical operator, that is OR. OR operator says that it will become true if even one of a condition is true. For example, imagine that you have a situation where you will say, I will go to the [office](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Microsoft%20Office.md) on weekend only if my manager calls me or my team member calls me. If any one of them calls me, I will go to the office. Now this situation is an OR situation. Here, either your manager is calling or your team member is calling or both of them calls. In all these three situations, you will go to the office. This could be a simple example of OR operator. So whenever you get into a situation where you have to do something, even any one of the condition is true, you can choose a logical operator that is OR. Now you must be waiting on seeing how we can use this into our SQL world.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/combining-conditions-with-and-or?u=76281980&t=185)** So let's just go to our PGAdmin4 and see how we can club it with our SQL. In that case it'll give the result as an empty. Why? Because for few records might be this condition is true, for the few records, this condition will be true, but there is none of the record where both of these condition are true. Got it? So this is how an AND operator can be utilized. Now let's say if we twist this way out, we say that, okay, if both of these condition is not matching, let me pull out all the products where either the product is greater than 100 or the category ID equal to two. So I will just change this AND operator with the OR operator. Now if I just run and execute this, now you see you got two records. Record number first has a price greater than 100, but category ID is one. If you see the record two, the price is less than 100 but category is equal to two. So in the first case, condition one is true, condition second is false. In the second case, condition one is false and condition two is true. But if any one of the condition is true, this will appear. Reason is we are choosing the OR operator instead of the AND operator.
>
> **[6:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/combining-conditions-with-and-or?u=76281980&t=372)** If it was an AND operator, we got nothing. #### [Introduction to data manipulation](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/introduction-to-data-manipulation?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript

And another situation. Imagine there is one product, which now we have stopped selling, and you want to remove this product out of your inventory. In this case, probably, you want to delete this record. This can easily be done by using the SQL delete statement. This will truncate your entire table. Now there is a shuttle difference between the truncate and the delete. Delete takes more time compared to the truncate. Truncate is much faster than the delete. So imagine that if you have a millions of recording of one table and you have been asked to delete all the record from this table, in this case, it makes sense to use truncate command instead of delete command, because truncate command will delete all the records to a table in fastest manner compared to the delete statement. There is also a one more command that is a drop command. Delete command, remove only the record, but it doesn't delete the table. That means the table structure still exists. Even if you go and insert the record into this table, you can do it, but once you drop the table, you cannot insert new record into the table because dropping the table will delete the data as well as the structure of the table. For example, we have delete and truncated the data from this FruitJuice table. Even when I... On the left hand side, if I refresh this table list, I still can see a FruitJuice table. But instead of that, if I execute this command, drop table,

> **[5:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/deleting-records-from-tables?u=76281980&t=331)** and if I just give this table name, and when I execute this command, this will delete the table as well. Now if I come down here on table and I refresh, my FruitJuice table has been gone because drop command not only delete all the records from the table, but also delete the structure of the table. So remember, whenever you want to delete only the records, use delete command, and when you have to remove the entire table itself, then you have to choose the drop command. Deleting or dropping is a very critical steps because this can have a big impact on your database. So you have to consider a couple of things before doing this operation. So things to consider. Again, the first thing and the most important, put a where clause because if you don't put the where clause along with the delete command, it will delete all the records. Another is referential integrity. Sometimes you are deleting few records which is referenced by any other table. For example, let's say you trying to delete the category records from category table, but in the product table there are few records which is pointing to this category records.
>
> **[7:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/deleting-records-from-tables?u=76281980&t=425)** In this case, wherever you make a deletion of this main table, ensure that all that child table does not have those record referenced. Third, impact assessment. If you are removing something out of your database, ensure that what impact it will have. So we do proper impact analysis. Backup consideration, because once you delete the data, you cannot recover it. So ensure that you take the manual backup and then you delete it. So that in case if you want to revert it, you have the option to revert. Lastly, another technique people uses is soft delete. In the soft delete. Instead of actually removing the record from the table, they keep a one extra column into the table and they will mark that column as a true or false. If this active flag or a delete flag column, it's set to be true. It means that this record is actually deleted, but it's a soft delete. So by this, we have finished our [Data Manipulation](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Data%20Manipulation.md) strategies. #### [Introduction to joins](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/introduction-to-joins?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/introduction-to-joins?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] We're trying to complete a puzzle but found that pieces are spread all over the place. It can be pretty tricky to keep the big picture when everything's scattered, right? Well, in the world of handling information, [SQL](../../Skills/Data%20Science/SQL.md) joins are like our best friends that help us bring all the puzzle pieces together in one neat picture. Let's understand with a real-world example. That is a pizza party. Imagine you are planning the perfect pizza party. In one corner, you have a list of guests, and in the other, a menu with dishes. Now, wouldn't it be amazing to combine these lists and know exactly who is craving for what? That's what SQL joins do. They bring your tables together, like the perfect pairing of guest and their favorite dishes. In the nutshell, SQL joins are used to combine data or rows from two or more tables based on common fields between them. The relationship between the tables are established using keys, which can be primary or foreign keys. Overall, there are four types of joins. First one is inner join. Inner join, it's something when you do, it'll try to written the records
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/introduction-to-joins?u=76281980&t=95)** that have matches values in both the tables. Left join. Left join is something when you do on two tables, it will try to get all the records from the left hand side plus the records which are matching in both the side of the table. Right join. Right join will try to pull the records which are matching in both the tables plus all the records which are there in the right hand side table. And full outer join, it will try to get all the records which are matching plus all the records which are there in the left side table, and all the records which is there in the right hand side table, irrespective whether they are matching or not matching. So at this point, we have just talked about all these four types of join at a very high level. As we move forward in this course, we are going to talk about all of these joins and we'll see the practical implementation of the same. Now before moving towards the implementation of the joins, let's understand few important aspects about the join. Why everybody's talking about the joins and why it is critical. Joins actually combines the data. Join allows you to merge the data from two or more table based on a related column. It helps you to create a single output table with combined information that could be pretty crucial
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/introduction-to-joins?u=76281980&t=193)** in many of the business aspects. Joins helps you to do the analysis. It increases the power of your analysis. By combining the data from multiple tables, you will get the detailed analysis and get the deeper insights of the business. Join helps in establishing the relationship between different tables in the [Databases](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Databases.md), which is crucial for maintaining the [Data Integrity](../../Skills/Cybersecurity/Data%20Integrity.md) and understanding how different data element interact with each other. Joins improve the efficiency of the [Database Queries](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Database%20Queries.md) as well. Imagine that if you want to retrieve the data by joining the two tables together and without using the join, if let's say you are using some form of a query or some form of sub-query, or maybe you are fir multiple queries and then joining in some custom way, probably that might not be an efficient solution. So join will help you to get that information in an efficient manner. Last but not the least, real-world application. Joins are widely used in real-world applications, such as generating reports, combining user data with transaction data in [E-Commerce](../../Skills/Web%20Development/E-Commerce.md) or in insurance company, or you can take any use case. This can be applicable almost in all the industry examples.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/introduction-to-joins?u=76281980&t=290)** So what are you waiting for? In the next video, we are going to dive deep into understanding how we can run the inner join and take the advantage of it.

#### [Inner join explained](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/inner-join-explained?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/inner-join-explained?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] An inner join is like a selective friend who introduce you to the people only if they have common interest with you. In the world of [Databases](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Databases.md), it connects two tables but only share the information where there is match in both of them. If a row in table A has no matching record in table B, that data won't make it to the final table created by inner join. Inner join basically tries to get the intersection of two tables. It tried to find out the common records between both of these two table based on the condition which you have provided. Inner join also helps you to eliminate all the unmatching rows. You want to combine two tables and want to eliminate the unmatched rows. That can also be done using the inner join. Inner join also helps for the efficient retrieval. Remember that. If you don't use the join and maybe you have a custom solution build where you put your own multiple queries to get the match data, probably that will not be an efficient way. However, if you go ahead with using the inner join, it will be an efficient way.

#### [Left and right joins explained](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/left-and-right-joins-explained?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/left-and-right-joins-explained?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Left join, as the name suggests, it is a join which has a biasing towards left-hand side Whenever you try to left join two tables, the result will be all the records which matched on the left-hand side and the right-hand side, plus you will still going to get all the records from the left-hand side, which are not even matched with the right-hand side table. So here the intention is we want to get all the records out of the left side, irrespective of whether it got matched on the right-hand side. If it got matched, we will get the values for those matched right-hand side table. If it is not matched, we get the nulls for those unmatched rows. Similarly, we have a right-hand side join as well, which we call it as a right joint. Right join has a biasing towards the right-hand side. Right join will try to [Fetch](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Fetch.md) all the records, which is matching on both the side of the table and it has a biasing towards right. So it pulls all the record of the right-hand side table as well, irrespective of whether it is matching on the left-hand side or not. For the matched record, you get the left-hand side column values but otherwise, you will get the nulls. Let's go back to our pgAdmin
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/left-and-right-joins-explained?u=76281980&t=96)** and see how we can use this left side and right side join. #### [Full outer joins](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/full-outer-joins?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/full-outer-joins?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Full outer join. As the name suggests it's full. So far we were talking about left and right. So left was pulling the left side, right was pulling the right side. Full outer join says, it'll pull the records from both the tables which are matching and the records which are not matching. So it would be like a detailed joining across to table. It is used when you have to handle the unmatched rules from both of the tables. Imagine a scenario that I want to pull out all the records from the customer table where they have placed some order or they have not placed an order. Similarly, I have to pull all the records from the order tables and we have to even find all the orders for which there is no customer matched. Let's see how we can do this using full outer join. #### [Using the UNION operator](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/using-the-union-operator?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/using-the-union-operator?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Presenter] You have learned how to combine different tables using JOINs in our previous videos. Now, isn't it amazing to see we can combine results set of different [SQL](../../Skills/Data%20Science/SQL.md) queries as well? We can do that using the UNION operator, with which if you have more than one query, you can get the result of these queries as a single result. Now you must be thinking what could be the use case. Let's say the use case says, imagine you have a lot of [products](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Microsoft%20Products.md) in your product table which belongs to a specific category. One, I need all the product names under the category, that is electronics. Plus I need all the products for which the order quantity is more than 10. How I can do that?
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/using-the-union-operator?u=76281980&t=94)** Let's just go to our pgAdmin and see how we can take the leverage of this UNION to get this two different problem statement joined together. So quickly just check, select * from categories. You will get to know that the category electronics has a category ID one. So I write something like this, select name from products where category_id = to 1.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/using-the-union-operator?u=76281980&t=153)** Now this will give me name of all the products which is of category type electronics. So in this case I joined product with the order. How? Let's do a simple inner join. I will say, select name from products p inner join orders o on o.product_id = p.product_id
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/using-the-union-operator?u=76281980&t=203)** and I will add the condition, where o., the product, total quantity, I would say, total_quantity &gt;10.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/using-the-union-operator?u=76281980&t=220)** Now you can see here what is the condition arrived. You are getting all the product name for which there is order exists because we are using inner join and for that order, the order quantity is more than 10. So if I execute this, there is nothing like that. So there is no record. Let's say I reduce it to just one. Are we getting some record? Yes, there are four records. Now I want a combination of both of these things together. So what I can do, I can say UNION. Now when we have a two different set of queries and we want to combine two different set of queries, we can do that using a UNION. But the most important thing which you need to keep in mind when you are combining two queries, the number of the columns and the type of the columns should remain the same. So a first query is getting one column, second query has to also give only one column. This number of columns and type of column need to be exactly same because you do a UNION between the same people. So let's just execute this and now you can see that. You got the record
>
> **[5:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/using-the-union-operator?u=76281980&t=313)** for the combination of both of them. In the category, if I execute, for the category, there are three products for a product for which order, quantity is more than one, there are four and if I combine them I will get all the product name which belongs to either of this query. Now another thing you need to notice is by default this UNION removes the duplicate because there were few records or the few product name which was there in the query one as well and which was there in the query two as well. UNION remove all those duplicacy as well. If you want to get all that duplicate also, you can say something like this, UNION ALL. When you do UNION all and try to run it, you going to get all the records in the repetitive manner. So here you can see that we are getting the headphones two times, right, because headphone is matching in both the condition. Similarly laptop, that is also matching in both of the condition and that's why it is appearing for two times. So let's quickly understand there is a small difference between the UNION and the JOIN. UNION tried to join two queries with each other. However, the JOIN tried
>
> **[6:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/using-the-union-operator?u=76281980&t=406)** to join two tables with each other. So that's the one big difference between these two. Another one is result structure. With the UNION, the final result is set of rules combined vertically, whereas JOINs combines the data horizontally to add more columns to the results set. If you understand in the JOIN, the column from both the table can come and that's why it's a horizontal. However, in the UNION you try to copy the value from both of the result into a same column. That is like vertical one. Relationship requirement. JOIN operation requires a defined relationship between the tables. For example, we are joining orders and the product. For this joining we expect that the order ID or the product ID should have some kind of, you know, relationship between these two. If I JOIN order with the category, it will not going to work out because I'm not storing the category ID under the order table, neither I'm storing order ID into the category table. So for the JOIN, relationship has to exist. However, for the UNION it doesn't matter because UNION is try to combine the query output. Use context.
>
> **[8:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/using-the-union-operator?u=76281980&t=499)** You should use UNION when you need to aggregate similar data from different tables of the queries. However, JOIN you going to use when you need to retrieve the information from multiple tables which are related with some common relationship. I hope you are enjoying the power of the SQL and the variety of option which is coming along your way. In the next video we going to talk about the case statement. It is an another interesting tool in the SQL world.

#### [Using the CASE statement for conditional logic](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/using-the-case-statement-for-conditional-logic?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/using-the-case-statement-for-conditional-logic?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Imagine we are making a movie about our data, and the case statement is like our script writer telling the characters what to do based on certain situations. So you have explored the vibrant world of joins, union. Now we are diving into the magic of the case statements. In [SQL](../../Skills/Data%20Science/SQL.md), the case statement is like a [Decision-Making](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Decision-Making.md) tool that allows you to perform conditional logic within your queries. It's similar to a switch statement in the programming language. The case expression goes through conditions and return a value. When the first condition is met, it will return the first. If the second condition is matching, it will return the result as per the second condition. And if none of the condition become true, it will return the else part. And in certain cases when there is no else part returned or no condition are mentioned, if in certain cases when there is no else part returned and no conditions are true, this case statement is going to return the null value. Let's look at the syntax of this case expression. So the case expression syntax look like, it starts with the case, using the when keyword, you will put on your condition, when condition one,
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/using-the-case-statement-for-conditional-logic?u=76281980&t=98)** and then you use a keyword called then, and then you write a statement which you want to return. So that would be let's say result one. Then you have another condition. Again, you will write that another condition using the when keyword, put your condition, again, then you use then keyword, and you will write the result which you wanted to return if this condition too becomes true. Here, this can be multiple condition based on your need. Lastly, you will put like else part. and once you write this else keyword, in this, you will write the result which you wanted to return in case none of the condition met. And lastly, you end your case using the end keyword. If you want to give some alias for whatever be the output comes, so you can say as and then alias name. So that's how you can put the case expression. Now let's take a very real world example. Then let's say I have another condition. I will say when price is greater than 100, and price is less than 500. I call it like an affordable range. So I will say that here, affordable. So in this case, if my price belongs to 100 to 500 range, we will treat that product as affordable one. And then I will say else, if none of this is matching, we will say that else, the product is very expensive.
>
> **[4:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/using-the-case-statement-for-conditional-logic?u=76281980&t=299)** And lastly, we call end as ProductType, okay? And now you can see that the laptop is coming out to be expensive, headphones is coming affordable, cheap. Let's do one more thing. Let's have this price as well. So you'll get to know what exactly is coming. So you can see the first row is null. So null is not matching anywhere. So it goes to the end, that is the expensive. But when our headphones, which price is 129, this comes under the affordable range, because it is more than 100 and less than 500. Similarly, backpack, you can see it is priced 49, which is less than 100. And we have this condition saying if less than 100, then it comes as cheap, great. And similarly for this, last, fitness tracker as well. So that is the power of our case statement. Whenever you want to have multiple conditions

#### [Filtering GROUP BY results with the HAVING clause](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/filtering-group-by-results-with-the-having-clause?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/filtering-group-by-results-with-the-having-clause?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The HAVING clause in [SQL](../../Skills/Data%20Science/SQL.md) is used to filter the groups of a data written by the GROUP BY operation. Think like that. You use the GROUP BY. GROUP BY categorize the data based on a specific column, whatever you give. Now, it will give you all the rows for that grouping, but you don't want all the rows even while you do the grouping or summarization. You want a summary for a very specific information. In that case, when you want to filter out this grouping, you can use the HAVING clause. And when you use this HAVING clause along with GROUP BY, many times you end up with using the aggregate functions also along with it. Overall, this will give you the fine grain control over the results which you get by grouping. Let's take a simple example. Imagine that your customers is located across the world and you said that, okay, I will group by my customer based on the city and I will get to know all the cities which has a specific number of customers. But I want to focus for my marketing. I don't want to focus on the cities where my customer count is less than 100 because that is a very small market.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/filtering-group-by-results-with-the-having-clause?u=76281980&t=94)** So now in this situation, when I wanted to know all my customer cities where the customer count is more than 100, probably I'm going to utilize the HAVING clause because I group the data and then I'm going to filter it using the HAVING clause. Let's just go back to our pgAdmin and see if I have to write such a query, how I can write it. So I want the cities, so I will say: "select city from customer" and then I will say "group by" based on the city. If I execute this one, I probably will see all the cities, right? And in fact, if I just say like this: "city, count", so it will try to find me all this customer count in that specific city, which is one, one for everything. Imagine I wanted to find out the cities where it is more than one or like more than 100. I would have been using: "having count of star is greater than 100"
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/filtering-group-by-results-with-the-having-clause?u=76281980&t=181)** and then I will execute. Probably here, I will not get any output at this point, but I hope you're getting the intent. So if you got anything from this GROUP BY result, and if you want to filter that out, you can use the HAVING clause. If I use a WHERE clause here, in general whenever you have to filter, you generally use a WHERE clause. But the problem with the WHERE clause is WHERE clause works before the GROUP BY. So if I was doing something where I should have to use the WHERE before the GROUP BY class, so it could filter out the record and then we're doing a grouping based on that. But once you've done the GROUP BY, if you try to filter on the group data, then you have to use the HAVING clause. So remember this simple small trick. The HAVING will come after the GROUP BY, and GROUP BY always come after the WHERE clause. So it should not be that you using the GROUP BY first and then you are using the WHERE. That will be a problem. So WHERE filter the record based on the rows. HAVING filter the record based on the group data. Let's take in another example. Imagine that we have a product and product has a category, right? I wanted to find out all the categories for which the product count is more than one.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/filtering-group-by-results-with-the-having-clause?u=76281980&t=277)** How would I do that? I will say that: "select name, category.Id from categories c join",
>
> **[4:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/filtering-group-by-results-with-the-having-clause?u=76281980&t=292)** our [products](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Microsoft%20Products.md) table, on, let's give an alliance to products, "SP", "on c.category.id equal to p.category.id."
>
> **[5:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/filtering-group-by-results-with-the-having-clause?u=76281980&t=308)** And then we say once you join this, we'll say group by based on category ID. And I say, "having the count of product_id should be greater than one." Now when I execute this query, let's say here use the c.name and c.category and here we'll use p.product_id. Let's say, c.category_id here as well. Now you will find out that there is only one category exist where the product ID or the number of product count is greater than one. If you want to see all the list, probably instead of running this, I would've been using this query. If I run the half of this piece, you can see that there is a three category: category one, category two, category three. And even if I add this here, product count, you will see the total number of product count for each category. You can see that for home and kitchen, the product category count is one. For [Electronics](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Electronics.md), it's three. And for clothing, it's one.
>
> **[6:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/filtering-group-by-results-with-the-having-clause?u=76281980&t=400)** So in case when I want the product category where the total product count is more than one, I would be using this HAVING clause. Why? Because I joined this two table and then I'm grouping everything based on the category. Once I group based on the category, and if I have to find out the filtered record from this group data, I have to use this HAVING clause. Correct? So once I use this entire query, I will get only the product category where the number of records or where the number of the product count is more than one. So remember, HAVING clause is used to filter out the group data and WHERE clause is used to filter out the non-group data. By this, we have mastered the variety of joints, unions, and various aggregation technique. In the next video, we will going to deep dive into another interesting set of SQL function.

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/introduction-to-sql-functions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Imagine you have a magical kitchen tool called a Chop-O-Matic. This tool takes vegetable as input, chops them up, and gives you a neatly diced salad as an output. You don't need to know how it works inside. You just know that when you use it, you get the result you want. In the world of programming, a function is like your Chop-O-Matic. It's a predefined set of instructions that takes something in, does something with it, and gives you the result. You can use this function whenever you need that a specific task done without worrying about the nitty gritty details. So, in the technical terms, a function is a reusable piece of a code that performs a specific task. For example, when you use calculator, you just give the two numbers to sum that up. You don't care how it do the sum. If you want to do the multiplication, you just pass two numbers and say multiply. It gives you the result. You don't care how it works internally. Also, when you write your code or queries in terms of a function, the code is easy to maintain. So function brings in the maintainability in your code. ### 6. SQL Analytical Functions

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Introduction to analytical functions](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/introduction-to-analytical-functions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/introduction-to-analytical-functions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] As you are managing an [E-Commerce](../../Skills/Web%20Development/E-Commerce.md) platform, now imagine you want to analyze the performance of each customer by comparing their order amounts with an average order amount within a specific timeframe. Here's where [SQL](../../Skills/Data%20Science/SQL.md) analytical functions come into the play. By leveraging analytical functions, you can gain deeper insights into customer behavior, identify trends, and make data-driven decision in your e-commerce business. Analytical, which also known as window functions in SQL, are powerful tools that provide advanced [Data Manipulation](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Data%20Manipulation.md) capabilities. Let just see all them in more detail. Efficient data manipulation, analytical functions allows for complex calculation across sets of rows that are related to the current row. With functions like ROW_NUMBER, SUM, or LEAD and LAG, you can perform data manipulations that would be cumbersome or inefficient with traditional SQL. Analytical function helps to enhance the readability as well. Instead of writing these subqueries or complex joins, analytical functions let you accomplish the same task within a single query. This make the code more simpler
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/introduction-to-analytical-functions?u=76281980&t=95)** and easier to understand for anyone. For instance, calculating the running totals or calculating the ranking rows does not require self joins or any subqueries. It also helps for performance optimization by operating over partitions of the data and avoiding multiple scans of the same table. Analytical function can optimize the performance. For example, calculating the moving average or finding the cumulative sums with window function can be faster than if you do the same thing using either the cell join or some other subquery way. [Scalability](../../Skills/Artificial%20Intelligence%20(AI)/Scalability.md). Analytical function often improves scalability as well. And how they do it? They actually reduce the need for resource-intensive operations, like correlated subqueries, and as your data size grow over a period of time, this function can handle large dataset very efficiently when you manage your tables' indexes properly. Another benefit this gives you is the windowing capability. These analytical functions, for example, like RANK, DENSE_RANK, they can leverage the windowing capability
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/introduction-to-analytical-functions?u=76281980&t=189)** by providing you insights that are not just row specific, but make you a specific set of rows or based on some context. They allow you to divide your results set into logical segments or the partitions, which we sometimes call as [Windows](../../Glossary/Service/Windows.md), and then you can perform the calculation on those segment of the data. These are some of the most common analytical functions. First of them, ranking functions. In the ranking functions, you have a function like RANK or a DENSE_RANK. RANK assigns a unique rank to each distinct row within the results set. Similarly, DENSE_RANK also gives the rank to each row, but without having the gaps between the rank values. Next window functions, there's a LEAD and LAG. These are two popular window functions that access the data for subsequent or the preceding rows within the result set. We will see all of these in more detail as we move forward in the course. We also have an aggregated function with OVER. You can apply the aggregate functions, like SUM, AVERAGE, MAX, MIN, et cetera, over a specified set of rows, which you got under the window. We also have a ROW_NUMBER function
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/introduction-to-analytical-functions?u=76281980&t=282)** that assigns a specific row number to each row, which you got in the result set. We also have a cumulative distribution function that can calculate the cumulative distribution of a value within a certain group. These functions add analytical capabilities to your SQL queries, allowing you to gain deeper insight into your data. This is almost certain that you're going to get drilled on these analytical functions, so this plays a very crucial role, not only in the SQL world but also into the world of your SQL interviews. So focus on all of these. In the next video, we go deep down into the RANK function. See you there.

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/using-the-rank-function?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] RANK function in [PostgreSQL](../../Skills/Software%20Development/PostgreSQL.md) is a window function that assigns a rank to each row within a partition of a result set. The rank of a row is one plus the number of ranks that comes before it. For example, like in an school, or in your college when you have an exam and the exam results go out, based on the marks every student get, there is a rank assigned to each of these student. Similarly, in the [SQL](../../Skills/Data%20Science/SQL.md) world also, when you are trying to analyze something and based on your need, you want to rank something, there a RANK function can be utilized. It is particularly useful in the scenarios where you want to compare items across some ordered groups. Let us see how RANK function works. RANK assigns a unique rank to each row. Maybe you decide the order, let's say, for example, if we talk about our product table. Now, in the product table, if I want to identify the product ranks based on the amount of this product, or the price of this product, so I can say that I'm doing a ranking of this product based on the price of the product in either ascending order or in descending order. Ranks are not consecutive. Sometimes imagine that if two rows,

> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/using-the-rank-function?u=76281980&t=95)** or two product price having exact the same price. #### [Using the DENSE_RANK() function](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/using-the-dense-rank-function?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/using-the-dense-rank-function?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] DENSE_RANK function in [PostgreSQL](../../Skills/Software%20Development/PostgreSQL.md) assign a rank to each row within a partition of a result set. The most important thing is it provides no gaps in the ranking values. This means that if two or more rows have the same value in the order by clause, then they will going to receive the same rank, but the subsequent rank will not be skipped because we have seen in the case of the rank function, if the two rows have the same rank, the next row will have a skip in the ranking value. DENSE_RANK will not do that. That is the major difference between the rank and the DENSE_RANK. Let's take a real world use case. Imagine that you want to analyze your sales data to understand the ranking of orders for each customer, but this time you want to ensure a continuous sequence of the rank will come even when there are ties. That is orders with the same total amount whenever occur, you should not skip the rank. For example, this could be an effective or important analysis you wanted to do because you might be assigning some rewards or points based on the rank somebody achieve
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/using-the-dense-rank-function?u=76281980&t=95)** and therefore you don't want to skip any levels because of these ties. Let's just go back to the PG admin and see how we can apply this 10 strength function. Let's take the same use case where we want to analyze the customer ranking based on the order amount. I would say select customer ID. Then this time I will say DENSE_RANK. Last time we did was like rank function, this time we going to do the DENSE_RANK function. I will say DENSE_RANK over. Then again, we want to divide it based on what we want to divide it based on the customer ID. So I would say partition by customer ID and ordering I want to do it based on the total amount. So I would say order by total amount DESC and assign a 1ES to that. So we call it like order rank. Also, let's print our total amount as well, so we get to know what exactly the order amount is coming in, how the rank is getting assigned. Now this time you will see the magic. Last time when we do this using the rank function, we got the skip in the rank, but this time just see here. The row number three and row number four
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/using-the-dense-rank-function?u=76281980&t=190)** have a customer ID one, and your rank is three because there is a tie between the total amount, 120.50. But if you look at the row number five, even though there was a tie at the row number three and row number four, even in that case, the rank has not skipped. When we have used the rank function, the rank got skipped and the rank was coming like five. See here, this was the result of the rank function. In the rank function, the row number five was coming five because there was a tie and rank skipped the rank. But here when we do it with the DENSE_RANK, the skip is not there. So that is a major difference between the rank function and the DENSE_RANK function. So if you have a situation where you wanted to showcase all the rank without skipping any of them, even in case of ties, that you go ahead and use the DENSE_RANK function. In the next video, we talk about zero function. See you there.

#### [Exploring the ROW_NUMBER() function](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/exploring-the-row-number-function?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/exploring-the-row-number-function?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] Imagine you are at airport eagerly waiting for your flight. In the crowd, you noticed a display board indicating the boarding order for passengers. Each person is assigned a unique number, determining when they get to board the plane. In the world of [Databases](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Databases.md), the [SQL](../../Skills/Data%20Science/SQL.md) function row number serves a similar purpose, providing a distinctive identifier to each row within your result set. The row number function in [PostgreSQL](../../Skills/Software%20Development/PostgreSQL.md) is a window function that assigns a unique number to each row in the result set according to the specified order. Unlike the rank and the dense rank function, row number function does not assign the same value for duplicate rows. Remember, row number is not a ranking function. Even if two rows are identical based on the ordering, each row will get a distinct row number starting from 1 within each partition. Let's take a one real-world use case. Imagine you want to analyze the sequence of orders placed by each customer to identify which was their first order, second order, third order, like that. This can be insightful

> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/exploring-the-row-number-function?u=76281980&t=96)** for understanding customer behavior, such as how quickly they place the order, or when they place the last order, what is the gap between these multiple orders and what could be the pattern? So it's a sequence. So row number gives you the sequential and it is not skipping any value, plus not giving the duplicate to any of the row. It allows businesses to track and understand the progression of each customer's engagement over time through their order history. #### [Understanding the LAG() and LEAD() functions](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/understanding-the-lag-and-lead-functions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/understanding-the-lag-and-lead-functions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Considering our [E-Commerce](../../Skills/Web%20Development/E-Commerce.md) database, imagine if we want to analyze whether the demand for a specific product is increasing or decreasing or it is kind of unstable. It can be understand by analyzing the change in total quantity of our successive or the consecutive orders. That's where the LAG and the LEAD function can be applied to track the changes in sequential order data. In technical terms, LAG allows you to access the value of a specific column from the previous row in that specific results end. For example, it could be a very handy when you wanted to compare the current row's value with its preceding row. Like you wanted to check today's stock price with the yesterday's stock price. There is also another function that is the LEAD function that lets you access the value of a specific column from the next row within the result set. LAG get you the previous one, LEAD gets you the next one. So imagine that you have a sales data and maybe you are trying

#### [Calculating moving averages](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/calculating-moving-averages?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/calculating-moving-averages?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Moving Averages. Assume that you want to understand the trend in daily temperatures in your city. To figure out whether it is getting warmer or cooler over time, you collect the temperature data every day for a month. Now, instead of looking at the raw daily temperature, you decide to calculate a seven-day moving average. This means, for each day, you take the average of temperature on that day and the temperature of the six preceding days. This smoothens out the daily fluctuation, and you can get the trend line. Here how it works. On day one, let's say your temperature is 75 degree Fahrenheit. Now the seven-day moving average for day one will become 75. On day two, the temperature was 78, so your seven-day average will become 76.5. On day three, imagine the temperature is 80 degree Fahrenheit. Now the seven-day moving average will now become equals to 77.67. And similarly, it will go on for 30 days. By calculating the moving averages, you get a smoother line. That shows the overall trend in the temperature
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/calculating-moving-averages?u=76281980&t=96)** rather than you focus on daily fluctuation. Because imagine, if you just look at the everyday number, probably you will not get something, good info about it. If the seven-day moving average is consistently going up, you can say that the trend is that weather is getting warmer, and if it is decreasing, the trend indicating that the winter is coming. In other words, moving averages is a statistical calculation which is used to analyze data over a certain period of time by creating the series of average of different segment of your full data set. It is commonly used in various fields, which includes finance, economics, signal processing, insurance, and many more, where to identify the trends and patterns in time series is important. ### 7. Views and Stored Procedures

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Introduction to views](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/introduction-to-views?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/introduction-to-views?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Visualize your scenario. You have a job where you need to get the information from different places to create a report. Each time you want data, you have to go to the multiple places, combine the things, and then make sense of it. It's like doing a puzzle each time. Without using views, doing the puzzle can be hard. If things change, or you need to update your report, it's get even trickier. It's like rearranging the pieces of the puzzle every time you want to see the full picture. Using view is like putting together your puzzle without starting from scratch each time. In the context of the [Relational Databases](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Relational%20Databases.md) and [SQL](../../Skills/Data%20Science/SQL.md), a view is a virtual table derived from the result of SELECT query. It doesn't store data by itself, but gives a way to look at the data in more organized and simple manner. Assume you have a messy room with lots of toys scattered around. A view is like a clear glass box that need to display a specific group of toys, making it easier for you to see and play with them without having to deal with the mess directly. Let's understand the benefits of using the views in a real-life scenarios. It helps you to make your reporting very simplified.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/introduction-to-views?u=76281980&t=97)** Viewers provide a simplified and organized way to access the complex data structure, making report generation more straightforward. It also helps to make your data more secure because you can control the access to a specific data while using the views. You can allow the user to query the view without exposing all the details of the underlying tables. So now you can imagine if, let's say, you have an order table or a product table and you don't want to showcase to the user that you have an order table or a product table, you just showcase a one view and all the columns of these table, you can hide it under this view. Another advantage is adaptability. Views offer a layer of abstraction, enabling changes to the underlying data structure without affecting the queries using the views. Imagine that something got changed at that table level. Now, you might not get directly affected because you might be using the view and somebody can go and just change that view. For you, everything else will remain exactly the same because you might not be pulling the data using the same exact column name of the table. You are pulling the data through the views so that make the things more adaptable. Views promote code reusability by encapsulating the logic
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/introduction-to-views?u=76281980&t=194)** that might be useful in multiple reports or analysis. Your views can be optimized by the database engine, potentially leading to a better query execution plans. This can result in faster data retrieval for a certain scenarios. In short, using views in the SQLs offer a range of benefits from simplifying queries and enhancing security to promoting code reusability and performance. These advantages contribute to a better [Data Management](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Data%20Management.md) and make it easier to work with complex database structure. Now, we have understood the views. Now, it's time to see how you can create the views in the [Real-Time](../../Skills/Database%20Management/Real-Time.md) environments. Let's dive in into the next session where we're going to see how to create the views. See you there.

#### [Creating and using views](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/creating-and-using-views?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/creating-and-using-views?u=76281980&t=0)** - Let's just quickly see how you can create the views. The syntax for view creation is, you use create view as a keyword. You give the name of the view and then AS and after that you have a [SQL](../../Skills/Data%20Science/SQL.md) query, whatever you want it to generate the view upon. Now let's go back to what pgAdmin 4 and have a one real world use case and see how we can create there. Suppose you want to create a view to summarize the orders for each customer, which shows the total number of orders and the total amount spent. Now this view could simplify the reporting and analysis probably for the sales team members who might not be very familiar with the SQL queries. So how we can do it? Let's just see. Let's create the view saying create view, and then we give the view name. Let's give the view name as customer_order_summary.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/creating-and-using-views?u=76281980&t=70)** And then we give the keyword call AS and after this AS whatever you're going to write will become your select statement. So here what I want, I want the customer id and for every customer I want total orders and total the amount he has spent. So I would say that select customer_id, count(order_id), we want sum right? So I would say sum(total_amount) and then I will say from my orders table. But if I do, it'll not give me the total sum. Why? Because I wanted to do total sum for every customer. So remember what we need to do, we need to do this group by. So we have to do this grouping based upon this customer_id. Now if I just execute this, this has created a one view, which is giving me the customer ID and total number of orders this customer has placed and the total amount. Now if I want to use this view, now I can say select * from this customer_order_summary.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/creating-and-using-views?u=76281980&t=166)** So now I'm selecting not from the table, but I'm selecting from my view, which I have just created. And when I execute this, you will see the result. So now you can understand the complexity of writing this query got hidden under this view. Now, later on whenever we need this information, instead of calling this query again and again we just go and call this view. We just say select * from this view. Now when I do this and say execute, I get this data. So here I got it, like this customer ID has total number of orders one, and he has spent this much amount of money. In fact, let's say I am looking for a specific customer. Here I can add the where clause on top of this view itself. I could say something like that. I'm looking only for customer ID that is one. So I will say that, give me this summary for customer_id =1 and I execute this. And I will get the view detail for a specific customer ID. So that's the beauty of this view. It hides all this complexity. You can reuse the view again and again and every time you run this view under the hood, the query get execute, you get the result, and on top of it you can do further more filtration if you need it. Now let's understand the types of the view.
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/creating-and-using-views?u=76281980&t=262)** There are basically two types of the view. One is your standard views or the view which we have seen. This is one of the most basic type, which is just like you are saving the SQL query. It provide a very simplified way where instead of running that complex query, you just go to that view and you call that view. Every time you call the view under the hood, the query will get execute again and again and again. On the other hand, we have the materialized view. Unlikely to the standard view, the materialized view store the result of the query onto the disk. So it means if you run the materialized view again and again, instead of executing that query again and again, materialized view just get the information, give you back from the previous execution itself. It will not going to execute your view again and again. So it'll be fast compared to the standard because query execution need not to be done. But at the same time the problem is, if your source data is keep on changing, then materialized view might give you the stale data or the data which is not very latest one. So if you have a table which is not keep on updating or changing very frequently you can have a materialized view and whenever somebody execute this materialized view,
>
> **[5:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/creating-and-using-views?u=76281980&t=359)** they will get the result faster compared to the standard views. Let's talk about the limitations of this view. One of the limitations of using the views are performance. Since views are essentially just storing the queries and every time you access a view, the query has to execute if it is like a standard view. In that case, the performance issue is there because you are re-executing the query again and again. Another thing is your normal views or your standard views cannot be directly indexed. This can affect the performance further wherever you have the large data set. However, [PostgreSQL](../../Skills/Software%20Development/PostgreSQL.md) offers the materialized view, as we saw. You can use that. That can improve the performance and you can even create the index on the materialized view. Views do not store the data themselves, so they only store the query, hence they don't have any ownership to the data. If your actual tables got deleted, in that case, view will not going to return you the result because the ownership of the data lies with the table, not with the views. If you are overusing the views or creating the nested views where one view calls in another one, that can make the database schema more complex and that will be a little harder to maintain or debug.
>
> **[7:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-sql-for-data-engineering-from-beginner-to-advanced/creating-and-using-views?u=76281980&t=455)** So if you are using the views, keep these things in the mind. Otherwise, views are very frequently used in the SQL world and when you work as a data engineer, there's a almost surety that you are going to encounter the views a lot. Similar to the views, there are procedures as well, which we call store procedure. In the next video we are going to talk about them. See you there.
